He was brilliant and poised to self-destruct somehow: very likely at the hands of his greatest creation.


"Joyce, you do know where this is going, don't you?"


"Well, Folimari-"


"I don't mean that - to hell with him. I'm talking about you. What you're doing here will consume you. I don't know how. But that is your fate. This is an old, old tragedy."


The buzzer sounded. Voices murmured outside. Folimari had arrived.


Helmson could feel his window of opportunity to exit the narrative neatly passing. He would bear witness to this all, now. What had been anger and outrage now slipped smoothly into pity and duty. He was Joyce's second, and he would look out for the boy as best he could - that was his responsibility.


* * *


The arrival was decidedly awkward and chilly. At Helmson's prompting, Joyce made Folimari and his entourage wait while he cleaned himself up a little more.


The old Dreamer looked more grizzled than ever. In the salon setting, Folimari's 18th century acidic mandarin shtick was flawless. Here in recognition of Joyce's more modern, urban neighborhood he chose to embody a Warholian vibe. Understanding context was a key attribute all Dreamers possessed to some degree, and Folimari was a master of character according to setting and setting according to character.


Folimari wore a severe outfit of black on black with only his fingernails colored, his hair in aggressive white shocks. He wore sunglasses and he did not remove them. Once inside, he did not respond in the slightest to Joyce's surroundings. His thin lips did not smile. He indicated for one of his followers to hand over something, and Helmson intercepted the gesture, taking what resembled a small black cube. It was a Rubik's cube as it turned out, but black on all sides.


"It's a puzzle. Feel free to solve it when we leave, if you'd like to see what you'll be up against. Call me if you need any help with it. There, I've shown you mine. Now you show me yours."


Joyce led them into the back room. Helmson chose not to go, not to see the creatures again. As they filed past him, Sam brought up the rear. Neither Folimari nor any of his accompanying eye-candy acknowledged Sam at all. Upon catching Helmson's gaze, Sam grinned goofily and winked, whispering far too loudly, "Psst, 'Elmsie!" He mouthed the words, "I am Folimari's second!" and then flashed two thumbs up as the happiest extra in the school play.


Helmson needed fresh air. He took the cube outside with him. Folimari's sleek black limousine waited near the door, the engine still running and a burly chauffer standing by it with hands like fat hams. He regarded Helmson disdainfully.


Helmson went for a walk around the block. He felt something on his cheek and brushed at it without thinking; wisps fell away. It was his skin; it was peeling as though from sunburn.


* * *


More and more as the day took shape, the neighborhood became increasingly like mid-1980's Manhattan as needed. He ducked into a coffee shop and sat with a cup by the window in wan grey daylight. The radio in the place played something era appropriate.


A ringing in his ears that begun after seeing the manticores still troubled him, along with a throbbing ache in his temples. He kept checking for nose bleeds, and he picked at the dead skin peeling from his cheeks. He stared at the Rubik's cube and his coffee.


Helmson was not terribly gifted as a Dreamer; he had no high ambitions, and he added little to the great works of Alexandria. He was content to do his small projects and to serve as a foil for those more capable - even Sam, who was more talented in his idiot savant way.


He did not anticipate being involved in these kinds of narratives, and he was not sure he liked it. Would he alone survive to tell the tale? Or would he be the one whose demise galvanized a heroic surge in others during the last act? It wasn't clear and this troubled him.


It troubled him so much that he did not notice the Vicarial spy hiding in plain sight within the black of Folimari's cube.


* * *


The spies, both Black and White, met within Joyce's apartment and swapped information. It was decided that White should stay while Black followed the cube. The harsh lighting of the Dreamer's studio afforded more opportunity for White to move unrestricted. Black flashed from clothing to limo to coffee to cube and now looked up at the sad, lonely face of the aging Dreamer. It recorded everything into transmissions directly to the Vicar himself. It scanned Helmson the feeble Dreamer not only visually, but for more intimate details, seeing in his face a life spent hewing away at mediocrity in solitude. A life lived with only enough understanding to recognize how little he understood. The Black spy streamed through all the angles of the cube, within and without, checking for where to spring to next. It was also aware of other options in passing, surfaces dark and shadows darker. It then saw a way into Helmson himself, as his spirit slipped into a foreboding as complete as an eclipse. It was there that Black took up residence, hiding in the freshly formed lightless pits of Helmson's brain, mere pinprick spaces, microscopic holes newly made by a close encounter with manticores.
## The manticore prince part 3

The Manticore Prince (Part 3)

The puzzle to Folimari's Cube was almost complete, and yet


* * *


Sam clutched at Helmson's charred remains, kneeling with them in his arms and sobbing, shaking with grief. There was no other sound, beyond his cries. All had paused to watch in stunned silence, even the air was still. He rocked back and forth with the smoking bones, smudging his cheek to them and sniffling low incoherent mumbles.


In the middle of the scorched playing field, this tableau held for minutes, seemingly drawn out into hours, days even, before Folimari breathlessly raced across the pitch, pushing past Joyce. The old Dreamer dropped next to Sam and struggled to pull him away from the body.


Joyce broke from his stupor and whimpered pathetically, "It's not my fault. I didn't know-"


He turned to any who might face him, seeking recognition, some form of forgiveness and escape from this smothering shame. No one met his gaze, he felt as though he were drowning.


"I didn't know. I'm sorry."


Slowly, the crowd began to rustle with whispers, and there rang out a hiss, then a boo of disapproval. Useless betting vouchers flew and fluttered from all about the stadium.


Soon others arrived to the field, medics, police, even some members of the council. Joyce was roughly pulled aside, and he didn't resist; his head hung limply to his chest.


As he was ushered past, Sam looked up through teary eyes, and his face contorted with a dramatic, profound awfulness from sorrow into a blind and bloody rage.


He spit, struggling to get up as Folimari restrained him.


"You!" His raw swollen eyes bulged with emotion, and he shrugged off Folimari as though shedding his skin, emerging larger somehow. He dropped dead Helmson to the ground and rose as a dark wave cresting, taller and bonier than ever, his hair wildly writhing like some soft sea creature. He lurched a step forward, then another, breaking into a run after Joyce.


"You!" He howled again, his mouth hanging open, his teeth flashing.


One of the escorting officers turned with raised hands.


"Sir, stay back and let us han-"


But Sam's face opened six different ways into lively wet matter that flowed around the policeman, pulling him apart as easily as runny eggs. Sam did not slow his charge, scooping up and through the obstacle. Sam's flesh bloomed about in wild arcs, consuming and erupting with the urgent, crashing violence of a waterfall. From his skin, slavering wolves surfaced then submerged, followed by a thousand tentacles wielding a thousand crude blades slicing the air, and then hands upon hands reached for Joyce with knuckles sprouting fingers sprouting talons sprouting scorpion stingers and fish hooks on fire.


He sheared through the other officer with an exaggerated gory splatter, like a special effect done with buckets and an excess of pig's blood. Sam had now spiraled into a maelstrom of flesh and destruction, spewing hatchets and missile swarms and volcanic geysers of acidic radioactive piranha harpies, lightning in all directions.


There was chaos in the stands. Sam rampaged for several days, all-consuming and unstoppable.


* * *


Helmson clutched at Sam's charred remains, kneeling with them in his arms and sobbing, shaking with grief. Again the coliseum heavy with silence, again comes Folimari knocking Joyce aside.


Helmson looks to the old Dreamer, his face broken with pain and sorrow.


"My friend, my sweet Sam. Sweet child, Sam."


Folimari knelt and embraced both him and the body.


At some point Joyce scuffled by in the custody two legionnaires. Helmson struggled impotently in Folimari's arms, yelling after him, "You! It should have been you - not him! Not my Sam! Folimari, let me go!"


"We have him, Helmson. He will stand for his crimes."


"No, it is not enough! It's not enough!"


And as it turned out, it wasn't. At the trial, his defense attorney made the case that Joyce was not mentally fit to be held to full account for his actions. He was said to have suffered considerable brain damage due to working so intimately with his creations, which were revealed to be toxic.


Convicted of some lesser form of negligent manslaughter, Joyce was remanded to therapeutic custody for treatment and further evaluation. The case was closed, Sam had no living family to appeal. The remaining manticores were destroyed, the war continued and Helmson spent the rest of his days frequently visiting Sam's grave and telling him the latest salon gossip. At some point in his old age, Helmson commits suicide on New Year's Eve.


* * *


Sam spilled over out of the arena into streets lined with the most experienced and martial Dreamers, they intended to try and contain him. He was now monstrously huge, gouging craters of asphalt into his mouths and pulling down the masonry and steel from the buildings as he passed. He was still thrashing in full tantrum, and all that he touched seemed only to feed his anger and strength. He spread like wildfire, reducing the city to rubble from one block to the next. Nothing they did seemed to have any lasting effect as he simply absorbed or negated anything directed to him.


It was only when it seemed that he might reach the Library did the council realize just how serious this was - not just a ruined neighborhood, not just a natural disaster, Sam had become a vast singularity of wanton calamity. Should he reach the Library, it was not clear what would happen. It was a gateway, as well as a repository; would he pass through it? And if so to where? And if he added to his being all that the Library had to offer, he would certainly become as a god, churning as Azathoth in the void.


The Dreamers were not quite ready see that narrative through.


* * *


Convicted of some lesser form of negligent manslaughter, Joyce was remanded to therapeutic custody for treatment and further evaluation.


Years later Helmson began to visit him regularly, apparently at peace with the past and gaining the trust of the staff until one day, Helmson swipes a sharp pencil from a lax attendant and repeatedly stabs Joyce in the neck, claiming vengeance through clenched teeth and spittle.


Years later Helmson visited him, having reached some inner peace over the situation and still feeling responsible for Joyce in a faintly paternalistic way. He said that it was too much to lose one friend, and that he was not willing to lose another.


Years later Joyce was delivered a noticed that Helmson had died, succumbing to brain disease brought about by his brief exposure so long ago.


Years later Helmson goes to visit Joyce and is informed that he has passed away, by complete chance and accident, he is told.


* * *


"I give up, Joy. I can't see a way to solve this. And we're almost out of time, the duel is soon." Helmson let the black cube tumble between them onto the table.


Joyce stirred sugar into his coffee with grim solemnity, "He's just trying to intimidate me."


"And you aren't intimidated? These all seem like perfectly credible outcomes to me." Helmson shifted uneasily in his seat, "And I have to say I'm not happy with how many of them see me dead. You're the one who challenged him."


Joyce picked up the puzzle box and turned it about in his hands. He twisted one side, and then another at random, "There has to be a solution in which Folimari loses, or at least suffers. He's barely affected in any of these, so far. Do you think he rigged this thing? Are these all preset outcomes?"


Helmson shook his head, "No, that would be crass. Folimari would not load the dice, as it were. He's too much of an egotist to cheat. That's what worries me; I don't particularly enjoy unhappy endings."


"His Dreaming is so complex. I wasn't expecting it to take this form."


"What? You thought just because you tinker so literally, that he would follow suit? Joy, he deals in concepts and abstractions, Folimari's Dreams are highly complicated existential metaphors. Truthfully, as clever as you are about your business - he outclasses you. I was frankly surprised that you challenged him; he was right about your creations, you know."


"But I thought all Dreams had form. There's a war going on right now, a real bloody conflict against a tangible enemy, how else should we fight it?"


Helmson, shook his head and reached for the cube, taking it.


"I don't pretend to understand how it works fully, I'm not as metaphysically capable as some. If anything, I'm a literal thinker like you. I make things, practical things. Like this."


Helmson pulled from inside his pocket what looked to be a large magnifying glass, though it was pierced through the center of the lens with a rotating dial that bore several smaller lenses of different colors, some stacked within stout metal cylinders. He held it up to Joyce and looked through it with one eye, now suddenly huge and blinking.


Helmson adjusted the device and squinted at the target.


* * *


The band, Folimari's Cube, had been touring cross-country for six weeks now, playing every other night at some new bar or local venue. The group dynamic was beginning to fray around the edges. Too many nights of long driving and sleeping in short shifts. Too many nights living out of the van - or more to the point, the van and Dave's fucking drum kit. But the van belonged to Milt, and Dave and Milt were brothers. There was no way around it: the Cube would have Dave as the de facto drummer for its entire existence. Or at least until they got a new van. Then maybe they'd fracture, have some sort of falling out over the cash split or some girl. Some boy. Milt was a fag, he would tell it to you himself.


It was in his lyrics, in the leer he saved up like gargled lung butter, drawing it up deep from within when they pulled into some mid-west hick town and he sudden turned it on for the truckers and the regulars. He was a 1000% gay then, like a zillion kilowatts of sodomy and he prowled those flimsy honky tonk stages and he stared down those men in cowboy boots, he pointed his love songs and lusty anthems past the girlfriends and waitresses. He licked his lips and teeth like fangs while his moronic brother Dave banged out the most mindlessly basic of beats, always with the same trite fill, always with same pit-pat cymbal crash at the end of each song.


"Fuck. Milly, man. I like the song - but fuck, man - get Dave to close it with something else. You're singing songs about blow jobs and angels and he plays the same way for both. It's just fucked stupid. It makes everything sound the same. He plays everything with the same goddamn four bars."


And that's eventually how they talked, even with Dave right there to hear it to his face. Because it only mattered what Milton thought; Dave was his dog and his brother. And I say eventually because at first it was cool; the shine of the tour was still bright. If there were problems, they'd work it out on the road, man. Like, this was finally the shot they needed, and it just took doing a few gigs to hammer out the kinks.


But Dave wasn't just a kink. And his drums ate up the space in the van; there was never anywhere to stretch out and smoke, or a place to fuck the groupies. The van could be obviously rocking on all fours, lit up with weed and sweat and semen and next thing you know there's Dave banging on the back door because Milt says it's time to break down the set and pack up for the road. And it's like, dude - Dave, you dumb shit, it can wait 20 minutes.


A couple weeks of this and a drum machine starts to look pretty good. A couple of weeks and the rancorous divide only deepens. Ray had his bass, and Ty could fake the rest with his synths - they plotted mutiny, hunched over eggs and cold diner coffee.


"Man, fuck this. Milly's never gonna kick him."


"I know."


"And if it's not Dave with his drums, it's Milly himself. That cocksucker-"


"Dude, chill with that. I don't have a problem with the gay thing."


"No! I have two fucking stitches on my forehead from a beer bottle! And how're your ribs, huh?"


"Pfft! Whatever man. You know he got it worst outta all of us that time-"


"I'm just saying I'm tired of going to these places and Milly's practically giving out lapdances, to these freaked out hillbillies, and then we get our asses kicked."


"That only happened twice, Ray."


"Two times two goddamn many, if you asked me. Which he didn't. Which he doesn't. He never asks anybody anything. He just does whatever fuckall thing first comes to his brain."


"Look, dude can sing. And maybe not all the crowds get his songs, but you see how well we play in the cites. Fuck these little truck stop shows, what's gonna make us big are the city gigs."


"I got songs, Ty. Me. What about that? He never wants to sing my shit. I gotta beg just to warm up the crowd. What about that, huh? You know - you and me, we could take my songs and-"


And just then the door to the diner opened, jingling the tacky ornamental sleigh bell and plastic holly tied to the handle. The two marginalized band mates glanced to the entrance along with the rest of the patrons. Just a fleeting thing. You have ears that work and eyes that register and you look to the noise and the motion around you. Nothing extraordinary about that. But the glance lingers and what catches the glimpse holds the gaze like some kind of tar baby.


In walked this gruff thug in a leather jacket, sounding his boots heavily, behind him followed a small, wan woman with ratty hair and sad, nervous eyes. One of her cheeks was yellow and mottled where the last echo of a bruise was just starting to fade. This new couple could not be more clearly understood by all in view. The woman did not stray beyond arm's reach; though man did not touch her - did not, in fact acknowledge her presence. Yet she was as clearly tethered to his being as though bridled and bound. He walked deep into the seating, passing empty tables and booths, only stopping when he reached the back. He slid into the far seat, with a clear vantage of the room. She took the chair facing him. He sat upright, legs spread, his hands as massive as stones and just as coarse with visible calluses.


Ty peered over his shoulder briefly then sipped his coffee. He wasn't going to stare. Ray was not so reserved, "Ty, you see that? The big guy?"


"Yes, Ray, I saw 'em come in, just like you did. Stop gawking. I don't feel like catching a beat down for free. Typically we play the music and get paid for that first, right? Isn't that what we were talking about?"


Ray seemed not to hear him, steadily stealing looks over to the back table. He cut and ate his eggs on autopilot, gesturing with his fork.


"What do you bet he works her over regularly?"


"Oh, you figure that out all by yourself?"


"You think she likes it?"


"What?"


"You know," Ty shrugged his shoulders and tipped his head about, "likes it. I mean, fuck, why stay with a monster like that?"


"Maybe she's afraid to leave. Maybe it's love."


Ray shook his head, "Dick of gold, eh? Is that it?"


"Ugh, no. That's not what I meant - you know what? I'll be in the van ... with Idiot Dave the Bongo Monkey: apparently the only other one on this tour who can go five minutes without referring to penis."


Ty skittered a crumbled bill onto the table, "That's for the coffee. Don't take too long, alright." He left, knocking his fist against Ray's shoulder brusquely.


"And seriously, Ray, stop staring."


* * *


Ty picked out a cigarette and lit it just outside. He blew smoke and yawned. There in the parking lot sat the biggest, blackest car he'd ever seen. It wasn't there when he came in - it had to belong to the goon with the punching bag. He checked around and walked over, ambling. It was hissing, still ticking and hot from the road. Something dark and watery with an unhealthy sheen leaked into spidery rivulets beneath it. He walked around to the back, and was surprised to see no vanity plates, just a nondescript tag from a couple states away. Where were they going, or coming from, in such a burnout hurry? There was no indication of make or model on the car, no manufacturer's name plate. The hood bulged like tight metal jeans, and Ty caught himself thinking this.


He spit out the last of his smoke and walked over to the parking lot for the bar next door; last night's venue. Their van bristled with tinny AM radio noise. Dave sprawled in the driver's seat, drumming his sticks on the steering wheel, lost in a groove. He saw Ty approach and he grinned, nodding.


* * *


On the road, the four got along as usual. Milt took the first shift driving. Like his brother, he was animated and manic. But that was because he'd scored some form of sex last night. He had the charisma and recklessness for this to happen regularly enough. Even in these unenlightened corners of the world, Milt was able to find some closetted, self-denying cock to play with. Dave sat with him up front and they talked about something from their childhood. Ty tried to sleep. Ray plucked at his unplugged bass, working out some dubious riffs. They were less than ten miles out when the big black bruiser-mobile roared around them and ahead into the distance. Milt arched an eyebrow.


"That guy is either compensating for a particular shortcoming, which would be droll and unsurprising, or he's gifted enough not to mind that he looks like he's compensating." His wry sneer belied obvious mental wheels turning.


Dave had to reason this out, "You mean like guys that wear pink?"


"Something like that, baby brother."


Ty squinted past them to the receding dark vehicle, like a roving shadow in the broad daylight, a hurtling sunspot. He grunted groggily and slumped back, "Forget it Milly, if that's who I think it is, Ray and I saw him in the diner with his girlfriend or something."


"Ooh, what'd he look like?" Milt leaned onto the pedal every so slightly, accelerating. Ray paused his noodling and chimed in with a snort.


"Oh, that dude? Yeah he's serious business, alright," Ray returned to his bass picking, watching his fingers. He was quiet for only a moment, then, "Ty, man, you missed the show after you left; I didn't tell you. So yeah back in the diner: the guy's just sitting there, big as Mount-fucking-Fuji, and his girlfriend suddenly goes to get up - like to go to the bathroom or some shit. And I'm thinking - well, you remember what we were talking about right? So I'm just busy thinking and drinking coffee-"


"What were you talking about?" Milt looked at them both in the rear view mirror. Dave turned in his seat to listen-in, a dim smile on his face.


"Nothing, just guy talk," and then back to Ty, who still looked like he was snoozing, eyes closed, "So I don't even notice until he says - loud enough for everybody to hear - he says, "Heel. Quiet" Just like that, can you believe it? Like to a dog or something. And she's half out of her seat, and people are looking. I'm looking. And she just sort of wilts back down. It was fucked up." Ray is laughing now and Milt looks irritated, his attention split between the road and the rear view mirror.


Ty manages a shrug, "Yeah, so? We figured he was abusive, right."


"No, no, man, that was just the start. So then this other guy, just another customer, he's with his buddies and so he says, loud too, something like "He should pick on somebody his own size." And they just grumble. It's like three dudes in a booth. And now everybody is hating on this prick. Staring daggers, right?"


Ty illustrated this with pointed fingers from his eyeballs, zinging them in Dave's direction.


"So was there a food fight or what?"


"See, I'm thinking: if there's a fight, or something gonna go down like that, I'll just bust out on the bill during the melee-"


"Ray you're full of shit - "melee", my ass. There wasn't any fight. I was right outside smoking. I would've seen it through the window."


"But that's just the thing, man. It didn't happen because she defused that shit in a hurry. She said," and here Ray licked his lips lightly, "She said: You boys mind your business. My husband is on a mission from God, and I am his hound. I will bite what needs killing and hunt down the coon through brushfire."


Ty opened an eye and looked askance.


"The fuck out of here, she did not say that. Nobody talks like that."


"She said what?" Milt asked, speeding the car faster along the little stretch of road.


"I swear on my Mother's grave that's what she said: mission from God, and she was his hound."


The van jostled along the road a little further in silence after that for only a few seconds, then Dave started laughing. The rest of them joined, because it was clear that Dave had no idea what was funny about what was said. It was his goofy ignorance they found amusing, and a welcome diversion from thoughts of the inexplicable insanity of the world.


* * *


Helmson cast down the cube in frustration and disgust. His fingers ached. His own mechanical dreamings did little to expose Folimari's construction, only enough to show that it was much more complicated than it first appeared. Joyce had been watching him expectantly and it was obvious that he wanted to ask him questions. Helmson removed his elaborate spectacles and the various lenses folded neatly away into each other, compacting down to practically nothing. He rubbed the bridge of his nose and frowned.


"Don't look at me like that. I know what you're thinking. Well, I should say: I know partly what you're thinking. I can't fully know, because you're a genius. Folimari's a genius. Even Sam in his way is a genius. Most everyone I know is a creative luminary, except for me. Your way of thinking is beyond me. I can't decipher it, and I can't decipher this." He limply pointed to the cube, black as ever.


"But what did you see? Was it about us? The upcoming duel?"


Helmson sighed and shook his head, "I don't know. I don't know what I saw. I told you, Folimari does not Dream so concretely as you or I do. He does not make things. For all your promise and potential, you have not fully elevated your skills into the conceptual. You can form wildly imaginative Dreams, yet they are so literal. You build monsters, Joy. But that's all they are, mere monsters."


"I make life," Joyce quipped, visibly bruised. Helmson rolled his eyes and sat up, "Yes, yes you do. Don't take my words the wrong way, my friend. Please don't misunderstand me. You do imbue your work with uncanny, credible, vitality. But you've had your turns with this puzzlebox by now, you do see that Folimari operates on another plane entirely. He plays with realities, not life." Helmson almost said: "not just life," but thought better of it as the words tumbled from his mouth.


"So? What do his visions matter unless they take tangible form? And if and when they do, then my living dreams will be on an equal footing."


"You still don't understand. You think too literally. There is abstraction to consider. Metaphor and grace. These are sources of power that you do not dabble in."


Joyce seemed petulant. It was clear that he wanted the satisfaction of seeing Folimari suffer at the hands of his beasts. It was also clear that such an event was highly unlikely to occur. This situation would never even get that far, or in such a form.


"Well, can you help me?"


Helmson, sucked his teeth and shrugged.


"I can try. That's my duty. Ultimately though, you will have to face this thing alone." He glanced down at the cube, "I may be able to make a few devices that could help you to see what he's doing, even if you can't understand what you're looking at. In the most technical terms, Folimari's dreams are concerned with semiotics; a field of study you would do well to learn more about. It is made up of three elements: semantics, which is the relationship between signs and the things they refer to, syntactics, which is the relationship of signs to each other in formal structures, and pragmatics, which is the relationship of signs to their impacts on those who use them. I may be able to help you to recognize syntax and to boost your pragmatic competence, but as for semantics - which is the most critical of the three - I can do nothing for you, as I struggle to grasp it myself. The others can be quantified to a certain degree, and devices can be made to help in assessment."


He stopped talking when he noticed Joyce wasn't listening. The boy was out of his depth.


"He's going to make a fool of me, isn't he, Helmson."


Helmson did not know how to respond.


* * *


Within Helmson, the Vicar's Black Spy peered out from one iris, then the other, observing and recording and transmitting. As the night fell, shadows lengthened and deepened. It would be able to move about much more freely at night, though the inky, petroleum black of the cube still registered high on its list of optimal havens. The spy saw the visions inside it, both first hand and through Helmson's eyes. The box was extremely prescient, more sensitive to narrative than perhaps even its creator could understand. It had picked up signals from beyond its realm, but the clues were lost on Helmson. Only Folimari himself might recognize the extra material, additions to his creation that he did not intend.


The Vicar would be pleased at the information, the open evidence. He does not deal in obfuscation, all his plans are bare and discernable. Indeed, such is his goal for all things: to be plainly understood. Alexandria would be struck down to smoking rubble, the heart of their power fallen even as the Dreamers overwhelmed his martial forces elsewhere along the front line.


    Turning and turning in the widening gyre

    The falcon cannot hear the falconer;

    Things fall apart; the centre cannot hold;

    Mere anarchy is loosed upon the world,

    The blood-dimmed tide is loosed, and everywhere

    The ceremony of innocence is drowned;

    The best lack all conviction, while the worst

    Are full of passionate intensity.


    Surely some revelation is at hand;

    Surely the Second Coming is at hand.

    The Second Coming! Hardly are those words out

    When a vast image out of Spiritus Mundi

    Troubles my sight: a waste of desert sand;

    A shape with lion body and the head of a man,

    A gaze blank and pitiless as the sun,

    Is moving its slow thighs, while all about it

    Wind shadows of the indignant desert birds.

    The darkness drops again but now I know

    That twenty centuries of stony sleep

    Were vexed to nightmare by a rocking cradle,

    And what rough beast, its hour come round at last,

    Slouches towards Bethlehem to be born?


* * *


The Vicar's Man-o-War has his hand tangled in her hair, his other is a closed fist - as it has been for the last hour, unclenched and busy. It reels out with the thrown punch and recoils, leaping away from him again and again, striking into her face. And then once more into her face. Again into her face, and then again. His knuckles are broken and they both look like travelling reddlemen, covered in blood that is equally his and hers.


She gurgles implausibly large glossy bubbles from the swollen pulp of her mouth. Her teeth are gone except for the molars, those dug in too deeply to be knocked loose just yet. Without a doubt, there is severe brain damage. One eye is punctured. Both lenses detached and useless. She hangs limply from his hand in her hair and barely twitches with the blows, reflexive spasms making her fingertips wiggle every now and then. His split face is neatly divided in contrast to the wet mess of her own. His whale sounds swoop low into meditative, long drones and subsonic trills below the loud and steady thud of meat onto meat.


He had pulled over the car to idle by the roadside, cut the lights and taken her head into his hand for this very thing. They drove all day, and were soon to arrive, but the events in the diner could not be excused. His has was broken now; he would find it difficult to control the steering wheel with only one good hand left, but this was something that had to be done. The balance between them was a delicate thing, and it needed correction; to maintain it required vigilance and discipline and occasionally sacrifice. They each had to be reminded of their place.


She was likely unconscious now. Her breathing was erratic. Occasionally other vehicles flew past them, briefly illuminating the red horror inside, perhaps the split-second of impact, or his hand slowly pulling back. People see what they want to see. A couple at the roadside, probably making out. Or having an argument, or waiting for friends.


He stopped only after his shoulder refused to work, his muscles completely spent. He could not lift his punching arm. There were hairline fractures along his bones and his elbow was numb from the force of the impact transmitted along his limb.


Her body fell to the side door. He turned her head down so that she would not choke on her blood, letting it stream out. She coughed and sputtered.


They were in this together. He had something he needed to do. She was something he required to do it; she cared for him in his frightened moments, and she forgave him his trespasses. It would be hard to look at her tomorrow, to look at what he had just done. But tonight it was dark, there was no moon, only headlights and the road rising before them. Beyond, in the night sky, city lights rose a glow to the ether, a glow about the horizon where the city lay. They would be there in mere hours, now.


* * *

    It was suggested that the Vicar might attack their capital.
    It was dismissed as an insignificant threat because the war was elsewhere, and their power was concentrated in the capital city.
    This is foreshadowing for people inclined to understand it. This is lost on people who don't.
    There is a box that shows you possible futures. Under closer scrutiny it reveals itself to be a metaphor, using one story to tell another.
    It is a receiver, like a radio. And it picks up imagery from other sources near it on the dial, other stories.
    There actually is a threat of attack, but it comes unregarded in another narrative altogether.
    The box suggests this openly, but it is unrecognized as such.
    The box points to the narrative as metaphor as metaphor.
    The outcome to all of this should come as no surprise, and it will be seen as obvious in retrospect. It was there all along. Only the Dreamers play with masks and subterfuge.
    The Vicar is concerned with bare truth and ruthless revelation.
    His agents of destruction are exactly as they appear; what reads as distressing is due to distress. It is that simple. It is not clever, it is not poetry.
    There is terror in the genuine, raw open communication of being. Not only because revealing yourself makes you vulnerable (and that is frightening for you), but also because what is revealed, unvarnished and ungilded, is the awful bedrock of existence, without niceties or artifice (and that is frightening for others).


The spies, both Black and White, piece this together from their joined information. They send this back to their superior. They themselves are watched by the Gray spy and she takes great pleasure in their flitting espionage. She reports to another. Black and White exist as imperfect echoes of her being. The Vicar is not God, and his spies are copies she recognizes as attempts to reproduce herself. She is one of the few females in this story, and she appears here near the end, because this is a tangled world of words and flawed men; other points of view would not be believed. She turns to you as a Greek chorus, and she tells you most directly:


"This one will kill himself; he is only waiting for the right alignment of circumstance, opportunity, and necessity."


She looks back to the narrative, watching the spies who watch for the Vicar, the eventual loser of his war. Black watches from within Helmson's eyes. White watches from the glaring lights of Joyce's studio. Helmson watches Joyce with pity and envy. His life will never be as exciting nor as dangerous as the younger man's. Joyce watches Helmson with creeping anxiety and disgust. The older man is weak and unaccomplished, without skill or imagination. Helmson will be unable to help him make sense of what threatens and dooms him. He is no adequate mentor, though that is his role. Others watch others, with varying degrees of comprehension. The Vicar watches all that can be seen, which is not quite omniscience. His sigil eye remains wide and unblinking.

## Collection: Myths

# Myths — Complete Text

> ⚠️ This file is auto-generated. Edit the individual files instead.


## The warrior

The Warrior

The Warrior was a widely known and much beloved figure, often serving as the protagonist in many of the high adventure epic myths told more to amuse than to explain. Notable is that the description of the Warrior varies considerably in terms of gender and/or age, and so perhaps a better analysis is that the Warrior was a general archetype rather than a specific shapeshifting individual. However, there are key commonalities to sagas featuring the Warrior that, when considered together, seem to establish them as a unique and specific character regardless of physical description. A distinguishing scene to all Warrior myths is when some offered temptation, bribe or fee is refused by the Warrior with the phrase, "Justice is enough." Another telling characteristic is that while the Warrior's martial prowess is considerable they never actually kill anyone and so the stories tend to involve rather fanciful punishments or, at worst, self-inflicted fatalities by the villains.
## The ladysmith

The Ladysmith

The Ladysmith lives in woods inhabited mostly by isolated Quiet, working his craft while surrounded by natural splendor, making repairs, alterations, custom builds, whatever you need. His location is hidden, but those who need his services can hear his hammering at the anvil for miles around. These few may follow the sound to his forge - if they so desire and can pay his price.

He will remake you into anything you want, as long as you bring him the parts.
## The hanging bull god

The Hanging Bull God

The Hanging Bullgod is both a general purpose weather deity and male fertility figure. Chained to the sun, he hangs inverted and masturbates fairly constantly, spewing out the clouds - and when particularly agitated, lightning. During these violent fits of passion, he thrashes about, sweating profusely and causing the rains. His chain rattles and thunders about, threatening to break. Should this happen, he would plummet to the earth, smashing it to bits. This makes thunderstorms moments of particular intensity and possible cataclysm; sex is said to be better at that time because of it.

He is also something of a peeping tom figure, enjoying his vantage point high in the sky. During periods of draught or on clear, cloudless days, people are encouraged to parade about the home naked as much as possible, kiss in public, flirt with strangers, make love on the beach, take second honeymoons, etc. in order to incite the divine lust.

A minor note is that there are a few small creeks and rivers said to have been formed by his horns grazing the earth. The banks of these waters are considered both parks and holy spaces, where courting couples are frequently found actively provoking the monsoon season.
## The stringeater

The Stringeater

Children that don't keep themselves tidy are warned with the story of the ragged Stringeater, a voracious demonic spirit that is drawn to all things unkempt and disheveled. He is described as a flying, corpulent, skull-faced figure, stitched together and bursting at seams with loose bits of brilliantly colored string. The Stringeater finds stray protruding threads on clothing to be irresistible, and a sure way to summon him is to ignore these threads if you find them on you - or worse, to pluck at them and make them more noticable.

In the myths (with two known exceptions), common victims are children that don't honor their parents, wives that fail to tend to their housekeeping, and the generally indolent.

The first exception features an otherwise noble and sympathetic character, a merchant's wife, who is preoccupied with arranging the happy marriage of her daughter. She takes on more responsibility than she can handle and is unable to complete anything, driven to distraction by all the unforseen details involved in the ceremony. When she proclaims to feel "as a cord, frayed at both ends," the Stringeater appears and promptly devours her. The other exceptional tale is of a tailor, skilled but lazy, who fails to trim and clip the loose threads on the clothing he makes. Each time the Stringeater appears to eat those who wear this shoddy clothing, they uselessly plead for mercy and explain that the fault lies with the tailor. Eventually the other townsfolk realize that the tailor is indirectly responsible for their suffering and they lop off his fingers.

As a result, the figure of the Stringeater seems to enforce responsible living, taking care of yourself and your surroundings, and doing your best, but realizing your limitations.

Few cults were formed around the Stringeater, and none of any serious political significance. Stylized likenesses have also been found as marks of quality on bolts of cloth as the figure evolved from myth, finding a lasting cultural impression as an icon of advertising.
## The cat and the moon

The Cat and The Moon

 

"I am trusting you to watch my cat that I have made. I have affairs that demand my attention," she told the Stringeater one night when all there was was night, endless and serene.

"The kitten is terribly unruly. See that it doesn't find any mischief," she told the portly Stringeater when he was much, much more than a monster for children and the unkempt.

"As you command," he replied and he took the small sleeping cat, disbelieving. It was barely a handful; just fluff and mist. It rolled over in his hands, bared its soft belly and yawned. Around the kitten's neck was a heavy collar and a thick chain.

"Surely this is a joke at my expense," he balked when left alone. "Am I not busy as well? Have I nothing better to do than to guard this small silly dreamer?" He said this for he had never seen a cat. It was newly made and without reputation.

It was not quite true that he was so busy. He had knit almost everything by now, sewn together all the pieces, and his work had been praised. All that was left at the bottom of his sack were his needles and a skein of the plainest yarn imaginable. It had no pleasing place in the rest of the pattern and he had left it out to fashion into socks, or perhaps mittens.

He was not busy, but his fingers were tired of fine work. He cut a length of this plain yarn and used it to tie the chain of the cat to his ankle so it would not wander away if it awoke. Then he sat down and hummed to himself, converting the yarn from skein to ball. The yarn was coarse and uneven; strong as dried gut in some places, delicate as ashes in other. He sighed and realized that it was so uneven that nothing more useful than a rag could be made. However even that would be something, for the motion of the act is soothing to one who enjoys knitting and lives alone. The skein was larger than it looked and creating the ball took many nights when all there was was night, endless and serene.

The cat woke to his song and stretched. It yawned and licked itself. It rolled and stretched some more and then purred softly to itself, still half-asleep and still half-dreaming. It watched the Stringeater who was then still in favor, an artisan of merit, and not the mere terror of children and the unkempt. The ball of yarn was slow to take shape and the cat watched it alertly, lashing its tail.

In the end, the Stringeater finished this chore and decided to rest before continuing, still unsure of what to make with the poorest yarn imaginable. It had no luster, the color was dull, it would not keep you warm, it scratched and held no magic nor charm to his eye. He set aside the ball of yarn and laced his fingers over his swollen stomach to sleep.

Instantly the cat rose to its feet, arched its back and padded over to the ball. It sniffed at it. It licked it. It bumped the ball with its nose and The Ball Moved. Pausing to groom itself, it thought about this new development. It batted at the ball with its paw, Moving It again. The cat swayed its tail and thought to itself about this further. Then it threw itself at the ball and tumbled about, pouncing and playing. Playing as nothing had ever done before. The ball began to come apart, fraying, tangling itself around the cat as well. All the while the Stringeater snored and slept soundly, fingers knitting at nothing.

The ball rolled away, out of reach and the kitten strained at the chain and collar which held fast. It padded back to the slumbering Stringeater and promptly gnawed through the yarn around his ankle. Now free, the cat resumed playing with the ball, chasing it about merrily and pulling it apart as it did so.

"Where is my cat?" she asked, waking the Stringeater. He looked down at his ankle and the kitten was gone. He looked around and saw that not only was the kitten gone, but so was the ball of poorest yarn that he had spent so long making.

"I do not know and I do not care. Your beast has stolen my finest yarn from me. I was saving it for the most special addition to the final work. Now it is lost and all is ruined."

"Liar," she said with a smile for she was gentle with the truth, "You were sleeping."

"No, I was thinking what to do with this, my finest yarn. It would have been my most glorious creation ever. Your own senseless creatures would have paled in comparison."

"Oh? And what did you plan to make? What more could you have added to this fine work of yours?" She watched him for his reaction and his fingers moved on their own, nervously knitting at nothing.

"I was -" he began, thinking quickly, "I was saving that supple and gilded thread - that sweet silk - to give life to the finished piece."

"And so you shall, I promise. I owe you that much for your loss."

He was surprised by her ready acceptance of this lie. Yet the Stringeater was greedy, and so he felt the urge to press for more. "It was my last yarn. I will need more of your precious hair to spin it again, you realize." He said this and looked over her myriad locks, now grown back from shearing. Endless possibility curled and tangled there and he was hungry for it. Her eyes flashed.

"You ask for too much, and I am a fool for learning from my own creation." She had seen her creature playing with the ball and learned the nature of Play; of this act that the kitten itself had brought into the world. She had Played with the Stringeater and was sworn to her promise, but she would not continue this game any longer.

She removed his face to remember him by and sent him howling to the earth of his own design. Mad, not knowing himself, he wandered as a demon. Endlessly he will seek his lost threads until the ball of poorest yarn is returned to him.

She found the cat, still chasing after the ball, endlessly playing with it across the sky, endlessly rending it into bits and snippets. She took these bits of yarn and string as souls and set them living about the earth, tangling with each other, knotting themselves and finally fraying to pieces in death.

She found the cat and told him, "You will push the moon and shred it to souls. You may do this as long as you wish for I have made you and your play does make me smile."

The cat sat and licked his paw to wash his face before speaking, "And when it is spent? What will I then play with?"

"It will be your choice to gather those souls again and spin the yarn to make the skein and form the ball that is your toy. When you tire of this, the folly of life will end. You may always choose to stop and the Stringeater will gather them himself and regain his memory."

"I care nothing for what he does or does not do," thought the cat to himself, "but I am enjoying this for now." He rubbed against her ankles and chased after his ball.

When the moon has waned utterly and all the souls have scattered onto the earth into babies and other new life, the cat pauses to lick and groom himself, thinking about the Stringeater, "I care nothing for what he does or does not do," he says, "but I am enjoying this for now."

He then collects the dead and the dying, often having to rescue them from the Stringeater until he has enough souls to spin into new yarn, slowly rebuilding his toy.
## The vicars adjudicator

The Vicar's Adjudicator

 

The Vicar's Adjudicator travels from town to town, but never actually enters, avoiding even the smallest cluster of two or three homes. He can be found camped by the main roads of entry, within sight of the city gates or along the outskirts of less formal villages. He hangs chimes to announce his presence and availability. He pitches his sword into the earth and uses its special hilt as the arm of a balance. He suspends two trays from this scale, into which petitioners are to place one of their own teeth. Against this, he weighs out a measure of consecrated sand until the weights are balanced. The petitioner may then speak at length about whatever comes to mind: family, work, friends, enemies, confessions, memories, hopes. Whenever a lie is uttered the tooth grows heavier. The Adjudicator converses with the petitioner, asking questions, prompting full disclosure and acting as a confidante. As more of the matter is revealed, the tooth grows lighter.

These sessions may be conducted in public or in private, but are considered sacred; none may interfere with the proceedings without violent reprisal. This includes interruptions not only by outside interference, but also by any petitioners who want to leave before the session is complete.

The scale tips either way until the heavier item is toppled. If the sands should fall first, the petitioner has said enough and the issue is considered closed. There is no way to predict how long this will take, it could be moments or days. For trivial issues this may happen in seconds. However, the Adjudicator will sit with any petitioner patiently until the matter is finished.

The Adjudicator is not a police representative and, as a result, guilty criminals may confess to him privately without fear of prosecution. He does not judge, he merely questions the petitioner and enforces the verdict of the balance. All that is required is absolute, honest disclosure. Occasionally, bold couples will marry in his presence. Each pledges their vows, hopes, doubts, deeds and desires to each other for the price of teeth saved from childhood for this purpose. Though what is revealed might not be wholly positive, marriages before the Adjudicator are profoundly intimate ceremonies and considered particularly binding. Loves declared are then known without question, as are all the faults and flaws. Indecisive petitioners faced with a crisis or dilemma can sit before the Adjudicator to gain help in learning what they truly feel, want and believe. He does not condone nor forgive criminals, bless marriages, or otherwise morally judge any of what is told to him. He does, however, require veracity; lies of any form will tilt the scale to some degree, and lies to ones' self weigh the tooth down quite heavily.

Should the tooth fall first, the sword is drawn from the earth and used to slay the liar, on the spot.

Tales of the Vicar's Adjudicator range from the cautionary to the humorous. In the best-realized Adjudicator story, commonly known as The Slow Noose, a convicted thief, facing death by hanging manages to slip his captors. He does not get far before they give chase, certain to recapture him. He stumbles across the Adjudicator who is resting beneath a tree. In desperation, the thief dashes out one of his own teeth with a stone. He sits before his confessor just as the posse arrives and he begins to talk about his sorry life. The novel proceeds to describe the next three days of conversation, recollection and flashback. At various points the Adjudicator asserts his authority over the restless lawmen, defending the sanctity of the proceedings and so on. By the end, the thief has grown as a person, uncovered the meaning to his life, and seen the error of his ways. He comes to view the Adjudicator as his sole friend. However, as soon as the sands finally fall and the waiting law moves in: he is hung kicking from the same tree while the Adjudicator watches passively.

Other tales are not so grim. Intended as joking anecdotes for adults, there are stories of children losing their first teeth and "squandering" them on silly conversations. These follow a specific format of give-and-take involving the listener, similar to "knock-knock" jokes. Additionally, the casual invocation, "Here's my tooth," and the traditional reply, "Tell me your troubles," indicates that the initial speaker has something important but private to say, wishes to vent about a heated topic, or is about to gossip.
## The feast of succession

The Feast of Succession

 

And see there now that the chieftain is dead and burning on a grand pyre of hickory and cooking wood. His horses are slaughtered, then butchered according to tradition. Their meat is ground with his ash remains; with spices and tears and wildflowers.

Any remaining good teeth are salvaged from the pyre and worked into the mixture, which is then fed into wet skins and twisted into sausage links.

These are grilled, cooked with onions and so on. A grand feast is held; any and all may attend and partake. All who do so must eat of the prepared food with with vigor and gusto. Wine is plentiful and the sauces sublime.

Of course among the sausages are those containing a prize tooth, and those who find such a prize are deemed potential new vessels of authority with the right of leadership. They must show their found tooth to all present, and they are allowed to finish the meal in peace.

However, when the last plate is clean, these lucky souls must immediately select among themselves a single leader. This is usually settled through duel, open combat, or suicide, but diplomacy, election, or other peaceful means are not uncommon. None who have not claimed a prize may interfere.

It is, of course, an individual's perogative to simply swallow a found tooth without remark, privately relinquishing any chance at power. And it is highly likely that those with ambition will sneak a scrounged tooth into their meal and claim falsely. Only the head cook knows for sure how many prizes should be accounted for; it is the head cook's responsibility to gather the teeth from the pyre and to knead them into the ground meat in secret.

All found teeth are considered equal; gold incisors, rotten molars, pitted or pure, worn stumps or sharp, weighty bone ingots. And all must be used. As a result, it is not unheard of for spiteful rulers on their deathbed to have their teeth drilled, hollowed out, then filled with pasty poison. Or to have them pulled entirely, refusing all responsibility for succession.

Once, a sickly but benevolent leader publicly removed all but one tooth before he died, in order to favor a peaceful transition of power. However this does not completely assure such a thing, as strange things can - and usually do - happen at the feast table.

When no teeth are available, for whatever the reason, the head cook must provide one of his or her own. If ever there are no teeth found, the head cook is held accountable for a crime tantamount to treason and is executed to take the dead leader's place in a new Feast of Succession. In one notoriously famous case, an overly cruel and openly manipulative cook who had climbed to power on the backs of too many spiteful enemies found herself in hot water when all present at the Feast of Sucession - down to the last diner - conspired to swallow their prize teeth in silence. Not only did this ruin her schemes at the last moment, but it stranded her to an obvious fate when no one produced a tooth at the table.

Needless to say, the head cook in the latter years of any ruler's reign is a volatile position of power and responsiblity, much saught after by those wishing to control or to guide the fate of the tribe. This intersection of cuisine and politics, poisons and promotions, transforms the kitchen into a battleground of intrigue, plot and backstabbing.
## The drought

The Drought

The drought had ground nastily at the wheel of the earth for two seasons now; it was an unkind, lethal summer unlike any in memory and the elders were desperate to appease the Hanging Bull God. He was a voyeur, dangling there from the sun, hanging from his hoof where he could see and know all. Sex usually did the trick; an orgy on newly plowed fields, on mats atop the temple, or in the shallows of the river.

But no clouds came streaming from his issue, no sweat raining down. The ball of fire rolled across the sky, closer than ever and hotter than hell. But there was simply nothing left to fuck and still the earth crackled and curled up into thick flakes of clay. The well ran dangerously low, the river receded to a muddy dank creek. The crops all died and the cattle sickened.

There was nothing left to fuck and still the Hanging Bull God was unmoved by their desperate, loveless efforts. It was an awful time. There was no love among the people. There was no usual rhythm of courtship and marriage, of union and joy. The priests and the elders had made it plain: their god was displeased and there would be no rain.

It was not long before the rapes began. It was the priests who began it, but then others soon joined them. It began as random and brutal. But then as many fled the village, those that stayed organized themselves; camps were formed. Endless victimization and long dark nights. There was no love in these couplings. Sweat was sponged off the skins of those in rut and saved in jars. The well ran dry shortly after the creek turned to wholly into mud. And there was simply nothing left to fuck.

Those unsuited to such exertion suffered and sickened. Among the very young and the very old, the frail, many died or ran off taking their chances in the desert. One way or another, their numbers dwindled, and those that remained organized themselves further.

In the end, the great Bull God was stirred by what he saw below and the blood ran to his head. He snorted out the first few wisps of vapor and thin clouds were seen upon the horizon, the first in months. Encouraged by this the rapes continued, the wounded were tended to with great care, only to be returned into position. A kind of unthinking barbarism gripped these thirsting people. In the end this must have pleased the Great Lecher for he sounded his chain like thunder and the clouds of his pleasure began to gather in the sky. The horizon was dark with his seed, roiling in vast grey masses. The rains then came. The Hanging Bull God shook violently, thrashing from his length of chain as though to pull down the sun he hung from. He shuddered and the sky shook rim to rim. His sweat fell freely in torrents and it was sweet and divine. He gouged his horns upon the earth and traced out swelling, flooding new rivers.

His sweat fell as rain upon the backs of those driving themselves blindly, mindlessly now. And it fell upon the upturned faces of those worn raw and whose ruined minds had also long since fled in horror. There was no longer any love, no urge to lust, nor the civilizing bonds of affection. There was only the rut and the display. So when the rains fell all collapsed in place and sobbed like the shipwrecked and the rescued at sea.

See now that from this drought come madness and a plague of bastards.
## Deaths herald

Death's Herald

I.

Huginn stuck his black beak
into sleeping Odin’s ear
and whispered,

“We know of worlds beyond worlds
and of ages long past into dust;
of places and times
without gods, nor mortals,
nor form, nor light.”

Odin shifted uncomfortably in his sleep.
He brushed the bird away
and rolled to one side.

Oily black Muninn nuzzled close
to Odin’s other ear and sighed,

“We remember all this and more.
We have seen all futures,
beyond Ragnarok, beyond time,
to the end of all ending -
and you are not there.”

Odin frowned, still sleeping.
His dreams were now tainted and disturbed.
He swatted the crows away
and turned once more.

Drawing his furs close,
pelts piled high against the cold
and the nattering of bothersome birds.
Nestled, he slept.


II.

He fell into a sleep so deep
that it was close to death,
so similar
that Death came calling.

“You crows, where is your master?
I may have business with him.”

Huginn and Muninn perched
upon the piled hides.
It was a mounding hill of soft plush skins.
They scratched and cackled.

“He buries himself away.”
“He sleeps now without us.”
“And without us, he will go mad.”

Death saw that this was true.
The All-father lay curled as a babe
beneath his covers.
A brooding gem in the heart of a mountain.

Death said,
“I cannot reach him this way.”
And the crows laughed wickedly.

“No! Of course you cannot!”
“There is no reaching him now.”
“Not until he wakes, if he wakes.”
“He will not hear us; what is he dreaming?”
“What visions?”

Death was unused to being denied.
This mindless sleep was troubling.
“I must wake him, if I can.
I must know the truth.”

The birds flew to Death
and sidled close.
They rustled inky feathers
and spoke in cloying tones.

“Must know the truth?”
“We know many truths.”
“Would you know them all?”

Death kicked at them
and they squawked into the air,
disturbed.
“Get off me,” Death said.

It was then that the mountain
of furs shuddered;
Odin was stirring.

“He lives!” Death said.
“He is dying,” said Huginn.
“He dreams without us,
and without end,” said Muninn.

The three of them fell quiet,
glum and moved to pity
by the moaning
deep below.


III.

Loki had been watching all along
as a flea within the furs.
He leaped up and revealed himself.
He said,

“Odin is not lost to us.
Come to Mimir’s well.
His eye is there,
down in the waters.

The well is a place of wisdom.
His eye is there
at the bottom,
so that he might see all."

And so they went,
with Death astride the trickster
now a mare, glossy and
rippling with muscle.

Death’s hands tangled
into Loki’s mane and
Death whispered close
to his tender horse ear,

“You work between my legs,
and I know your reputation.
Beware, I will have you one day
when your time has come.”

Loki swore to seek no mischief,
and his hair streaked white
in Death’s grip.

Steed and rider arrived
to find the crows already
crouched around the well.
They all gathered close to peer within.

“We see it.”
“There in the deep.”
“But we cannot fly to it.”
Death pointed to Loki,

“Become a fish and fetch us the eye.”

Loki became a sleek salmon.
He flashed through the air,
diving with a wet plop
down, down into the water.


IV.

After some time they began to worry.
Was this treachery,
or misfortune?
Death saw a mouse and had an idea.

“Mouse, drown yourself in the well
so I may come to collect you at the bottom.”
The mouse trembled with fear,
but he shook his head, saying,

“I cannot; my family waits for me.
We are small and far from home.
I must return to protect them.”

Death was not to be denied twice,
“You are terrified
and will soon die from fright;
I will have you either way.

Die in the well,
so that I may enter.
Do this for me and
your family will mourn a hero.”

“Yes,” said Huginn,
crowding close to the mouse.
“Yes,” said Muninn,
doing much the same.

“We know this to be true;
you will do your kind
a great service.”

The mouse’s heart beat painfully fast.
Death promised,
“You will not suffer.”

The small mouse nodded dumbly.
He was just a mouse after all.
He did not struggle when the
crows flew him over the well

And laughing,
they dropped him in with
a wet plop.


V.

The mouse could not swim.
He clawed at the water uselessly,
seeing Death and the birds looking
down at him, waiting and watching.

They receded up and away,
and he felt nothing but
the cold water all around,
until it was completely dark.

He panicked and bubbled out his breath.
Tumbling and sinking and falling,
he trailed blood and bubbles.
And still further down he went.

Strange creatures brushed his legs;
serpents with needle-sharp
teeth like glass.

He thought of his family;
his sons were still infants,
his wife again pregnant.
How would they know what happened?

He gulped out his last,
and Death joined him.
Death held his hand
and they continued down together.

The mouse was now unafraid.
Smoothly they dropped
through dark water
like two stars falling.


VI.

Later, they saw Odin’s forsaken eye,
small and distant below.
They saw the eye left behind
as the price for knowledge.

As they drifted to it,
Death saw Loki wriggling desperately.
He had tried to betray them,
to take the eye for himself.

“Death, help me! I am blind!”

Death did not answer.

“I plucked my eye to take his own,
but he is mad - what I saw was horrible.
I cast it from me, but I could still see!
I plucked my other, but I could still see!”

Loki churned and roiled,
streaming blood and bubbles.
He cried and wailed
and worked his mouth.

Death and the mouse settled lightly
to the bottom of the well.
The eye was there, clouded,
softly swollen and discarded.

Together they held it,
and Death whispered close,
“You are dreaming,
you are dreaming.”

The unblinking eye saw everything.
Odin was not dead, only sleeping.
Odin gasped; he shrugged off his covers,
and awoke.

## Coffee

Coffee

"I never liked him anyway," John says to me, slouching back in the club chair and pointedly hooking one leg over the other in a way that showed his hip and flank to me. Pleated khakis. Here in the coffee shop, with vaguely indie music all around and plush chairs by the windows, he guestures to me with his espresso, "You could've done so much better." He sips, thin lips slurping. "And it was - what, only two or three months, right?" He sips again, noisily, squinting his eyes at me over the rim of the paper cup with his coffee. Finally he set it down. This place has coffee tables. Long low things; at the far end is a man with a laptop and a stack of well-worn spiral notebooks. We are all modern men in here. Even the woman at the counter is suspect. John had proposed this theory that she was hired for her androgyny and cool manner. No smiles, short hair, flat chest, bold hard glasses, young. In this part of town no one flirted with her. John's theory was that she was hired for those on the cusp.

"To get them in here," he had said that time, "You know, the closet cases." That day he was sitting in the same seat, though perched at the edge, leaning over the coffee table to eat his scone off a small paper plate. He broke off a chunk and pointed with it at her. "It's not like us locals have a choice; it's the only place around. No, she's here to pull in the guys from the suburbs. They read an article in The Week, see a review and finally work up the courage to lie to the wife or themselves of whatever." He paused to smirk and to take another mouthful. "They drive by, see the pride flag out front and us old wolves in the window and almost turn around. But then, she's there - giving the doorway a quick sweep or shutting the cash drawer shut with her belly because her hands are full - and they come on in." He finished this and his scone at the same time, dusting his hands together with a flourish.

"You're full of shit, John. That's what I think," I had said. He looked both amused and pained. "No, hear me out," he continued, "They come in and see her: wife, daughter, girl next door, right? But then she also looks like that one guy they remember from high school, or the paperboy, or-"

"Or maybe she was hired to give you a woman to serve you coffee. Maybe you always want to come here because you can then lord your pendulous male dominion over her narrow hips." And that could have been true. John ordered his purchases as declarations: "The scone, there," or "I'll have an espresso."

I suspected even then that the truth of her boyish charm and sullen manner was something else entirely. However, for consideration - for casual conversation - we were obliged to keep things on the shallow, catty level of mundane and mild genial sadism between friends.

"I 'always' want to come here because I do not consider Dunkin' Donuts to be a viable alternative." That was said with some edge to it and we both let the subject drop.

"We met in January; three months ago."
## Goldilocks

Goldilocks

Always the woods, in her dreams that's where she was. And they were lovely, dark and deep.

In a dress that wasn't hers, with hair like Morticia Adams but blonde. Impossibly blonde, bleached and fried and splitting.

Dirt under her fingernails, holes in her socks, mosquitos all around. Her lips were chapped and cracking and she had sores in her mouth.

But it was cool in the forest and darkly green. It always seemed to have just rained, the soil moist.

These are the images that came back to her long after her alarm clock, long into the dreary daily routine. When drowsing on the train, unable to really sleep because of all the strangers and because she might miss her stop, the fleeting rememberances would come back to her then. In the rumbling black stretches of the subway, the world beyond the window grime was always the woods from her dreams.

She leaned her head against the glass and her breath misted the pane. She had several more stops until she was home, and then a walk few blocks more to home. Her apartment, her cat, dying plants, frightening news, inane sitcoms, crap food and a date with her fingers before something like sleep. Sleep and a return to the woods.

Her own hair was short and mousy. "Brunette" is the fancy word for it. She closed her eyes on the train and it rumbled through the earth beneath the city. Between stations it was dark and the passing shadows of the tunnel walls poured before her bleary eyes. Her breath bloomed and receded on the glass.

She is in the woods, and her dress is ridiculous, a tacky blue babydoll outfit that makes her look like Alice in Wonderland. It's marred and muddy from the woods. It is polyester and it itches, it makes her sweat. There in the distance is a small thatch roofed house.

The next station emerges and the train breaks, squealing. People come and go and she has to make room for a young mother with her brood. She's pregnant and holding a baby in one arm, holding a little boy by the hand. Fortunately this guy gets up and gives her a seat. The boy stands between her knees.

He looks across to her with wide empty eyes and the train lurches and shudders back into the tunnel; the black and the forest is there again.

The little hut is trashed inside, furniture splintered, shattered ceramics, cloth torn to rags. There is slop on the floor, old food or old vomit; it's hard to tell. An overturned kettle in the hearth, rotting onions. Clawed marks as wide as her shoulders. And then she hears the rains return outside, and she is trapped here in this place.

The little boy watches across the train and she tries to smile at him. He doesn't respond and she gives up, rubbing her temples. It is raining outside. The train emerges from the tunnels, shooting out onto an elevated track and she's floating inside it. Grey and rainy in the city. No more tunnels, no more forest. But the rain seems to have followed her from her half-dream.

At her stop she leaves, feeling the child's eyes on her the whole way. He even turns to see her her as the doors close and the subway pulls away, his small face smaller and smaller and gone.

She makes it home, her cat complains and rubs her ankles. Her phone is blinking and the messages are all the same; her mother, her ex, bill collectors, the heavy breather. His was the last and she knew it was him from the familiar thick gurglings. For the past few weeks he had started calling and she was simply too exhausted to do anything about it. She didn't encourage him and she'd actually never heard him live. But lately these raspy breathings on her voice mail. Sick, but it was attention. Her ex just wanted his things: when would be a good time? When indeed. She was too tired to deal with any of it. Cat food in the bowl. Passing out on the couch with the tv on, in her underwear and her tanktop and soon she was in the cool woods again, in the rain, in that house with the broken windows and the shattered furniture.

The dress was frilly and in the way, but she couldn't seem to get it off. She looked around and licked her dry lips, sniffling; she was catching a cold. It was damp in here, musty and moldy. the rain fell heavily, opressive, making everything outside either muddy and black or vibrantly green, lush and cold. There were beds, three of them, just like from the storybooks. And the frames had been destroyed, the matresses ripped open, scattered and befouled. It stank. She sat in a corner and hugged her knees, shivering and waiting for the rain to stop or for the bears to return.

And that was her dream, that was always her dream. Lately at least: in the woods, in the rain, the house, the wreckage and the chill, waiting for her bears to come home and to find her. It was all she saw in the night when she slept. Her mother thought she was just stressed and that going to confession was the usual answer. She brought home beer and that didn't help. She just slept longer and dreamed the same dream until her cat licked her face in hunger, her cat that she couldn't remember getting anymore. And her plants in the window turning brown and brittle, day by day.

At least she had a view. She lingered in the window and drank and until she felt self-conscious. Her breather might be someone in one of those other open windows, he might be watching her. She didn't see anybody else in any other window, but the thought remained and she sat back on her couch.

When the phone rang late one night, past her mother's bedtime, she knew it was him on the other line. Noone else would call, who did she know that would call? She didn't even need to say hello. She didn't say anything and they stayed on the phone like that while she walked over to the window. He swallowed noisily and huffed into the phone as if struggling with something. She listened for a while and then sat on the floor, pulling her cat into her lap and she told him of her dream.

"I can't sleep. No, that's not it, I sleep and I guess I sleep fine, but I always wake up tired and I keep having thing same dream of being in these fucking woods.. Like fairy tale trees and everything.. and I don't know. It's raining and I'm in this hideous outfit. And I'm waiting for the end to come, I've made a mess of things and I'm just waiting for the bears to come eat me," her breather hung up on her and she didn't hear from him for a week. But the dreams continued, and the house there in the woods fell to ruin more violently each time she found it. Doors wrenched from their hinges, floorboards split and smashed through. It afforded less and less shelter from the rains but there was nothing else and nowhere else to go when the downpour fell. Just mud and muck and chill.

A man moved in across the hall, she saw him unloading his things with the help of some of his friends. She did not make it inside soon enough,

"Hey there! You live there? Sorry my stuff is all piled up here in the way - just moving in," his smile was broad and he stated the obvious with a warm, easy-going manner that put her on edge. She nodded and locked herself into her apartment. When her breather called she told him about the new guy, then some more about her dream. It was confession after all. He called and grunted into her ear and she listened before talking, and then she talked until he hung up. It happened naturally like that for a while until she began to look forward to it, the human contact. Sleeping on the train, talking on the phone, walking softly by the door of the apartment next to hers.

It was during one of these sessions, after he was done and she was talking and crying as she sometimes did, that he interrupted her, in a wheezing flabby tone,

"Why don't you just go talk to him?" She hung up, then. That was too much. And she didn't answer the phone after that. She unplugged it after that. She drew her shades down and slept alone with her cat.

Eventually the house in her dreams fell to rubble. She sat beside what was left of the chimney in the rain and coughed and sneezed. No bears ever came calling.
## Here is my tooth

Here Is My Tooth
 

"Here is my tooth," she said, looking around nervously before taking her seat. They were outside, by the light of the full moon. Under the cork tree and within sight of the farm, in the distance, on the plain. Grasses as waters rippling in the breeze.

"Tell me your troubles," he replied evenly, weighing out the balance. In the cold light, the tooth sparkled like a hard and precious stone.

Oh, but no it wasn't quite like that. There by the window they sat across from each other and the city whirred along to itself beyond the broad glass pane. They had been meeting every week for the past month now: coffee and confession.

It was a chance encounter that first time. It was crowded and humid inside, bitterly cold outside. The snow drove in even those who didn't like coffee, the herbal tea drinkers and the alcoholics. The homeless kept near the door with their bags, ready to bolt if need be. But it was so very cold and icy outside. She waited in line before him. Got her coffee and a lucky seat by the broad window as a couple was leaving. He was behind her and she hadn't noticed at first. But there he stood with his own drink in hand, a newspaper under his arm, looking around lost for another place to sit. There wasn't one, of course. It was crowded from the snowstorm and so on. But their eyes met and he smiled and she returned it without thinking. She looked away but by then he was there,

"Um, so do you mind if I sit here?"

She blinked as if from a dream and shook her head, pulling in closer to herself, her bag in her lap, her own cup on the table between them.

"No. Here, please." He settled in.

"Thanks, it's just so crowded, I didn't see any other open tables. Some weather, huh?" He smiled and unwound a knitted scarf, crudely knitted.
